   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Moons of Neptune
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Voyager_2_Neptune_and_Triton.jpg">
         <img alt="Two large partially illuminated spherical bodies: a large one at the top and a small one below it. The light is coming from the left making the bodies look like the waxing crescent moon." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Voyager_2_Neptune_and_Triton.jpg/300px-Voyager_2_Neptune_and_Triton.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Voyager_2_Neptune_and_Triton.jpg/450px-Voyager_2_Neptune_and_Triton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Voyager_2_Neptune_and_Triton.jpg/600px-Voyager_2_Neptune_and_Triton.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Voyager_2_Neptune_and_Triton.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">
          Neptune
         </a>
         (top) and
         <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">
          Triton
         </a>
         (bottom), three days after the
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">
           Voyager 2
          </a>
         </i>
         flyby in 1989
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">
        Neptune
       </a>
       has 14 known
       <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">
        moons
       </a>
       , which are named for minor
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_water_deities" title="List of water deities">
        water deities
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">
        Greek mythology
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [note 1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By far the largest of them is
       <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">
        Triton
       </a>
       , discovered by
       <a href="/wiki/William_Lassell" title="William Lassell">
        William Lassell
       </a>
       on October 10, 1846, just 17 days after the discovery of Neptune itself; over a century passed before the discovery of the second natural satellite,
       <a href="/wiki/Nereid_(moon)" title="Nereid (moon)">
        Nereid
       </a>
       . Neptune's outermost moon
       <a href="/wiki/Neso_(moon)" title="Neso (moon)">
        Neso
       </a>
       , which has an orbital period of about 26
       <a href="/wiki/Julian_year_(astronomy)" title="Julian year (astronomy)">
        Julian years
       </a>
       , orbits further from its planet than any other moon in the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Triton is unique among moons of
       <a href="/wiki/Planet#Planetary-mass_objects" title="Planet">
        planetary mass
       </a>
       in that its orbit is
       <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_and_prograde_motion" title="Retrograde and prograde motion">
        retrograde
       </a>
       to Neptune's rotation and
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">
        inclined
       </a>
       relative to Neptune's equator, which suggests that it did not form in orbit around Neptune but was instead gravitationally captured by it. The next-largest irregular satellite in the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
        Saturn
       </a>
       's moon
       <a href="/wiki/Phoebe_(moon)" title="Phoebe (moon)">
        Phoebe
       </a>
       , has only 0.03% of Triton's mass. The capture of Triton, probably occurring some time after Neptune formed a satellite system, was a catastrophic event for Neptune's original satellites, disrupting their orbits so that they collided to form a rubble disc. Triton is massive enough to have achieved
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">
        hydrostatic equilibrium
       </a>
       and to retain a thin atmosphere capable of forming clouds and hazes.
      </p>
      <p>
       Inward of Triton are seven small
       <a href="/wiki/Regular_moon" title="Regular moon">
        regular satellites
       </a>
       , all of which have
       <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_and_prograde_motion" title="Retrograde and prograde motion">
        prograde orbits
       </a>
       in planes that lie close to Neptune's equatorial plane; some of these orbit among
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Neptune" title="Rings of Neptune">
        Neptune's rings
       </a>
       . The largest of them is
       <a href="/wiki/Proteus_(moon)" title="Proteus (moon)">
        Proteus
       </a>
       . They were re-accreted from the rubble disc generated after Triton's capture after the Tritonian orbit became circular. Neptune also has six more outer
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irregular_satellite" title="Irregular satellite">
        irregular satellites
       </a>
       other than Triton, including Nereid, whose orbits are much farther from Neptune and at high inclination: three of these have prograde orbits, while the remainder have retrograde orbits. In particular, Nereid has an unusually close and eccentric orbit for an irregular satellite, suggesting that it may have once been a regular satellite that was significantly perturbed to its current position when Triton was captured. The two outermost Neptunian irregular satellites,
       <a href="/wiki/Psamathe_(moon)" title="Psamathe (moon)">
        Psamathe
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Neso_(moon)" title="Neso (moon)">
        Neso
       </a>
       , have the largest orbits of any natural satellites discovered in the Solar System to date.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Neptune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tritonian_sky.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="692" data-file-width="1024" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Tritonian_sky.jpg/220px-Tritonian_sky.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Tritonian_sky.jpg/330px-Tritonian_sky.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Tritonian_sky.jpg/440px-Tritonian_sky.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tritonian_sky.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Simulated view of Neptune in the
         <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_skies" title="Extraterrestrial skies">
          hypothetical sky
         </a>
         of Triton
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">
        Discovery
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Neptune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Discovery">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">
        Triton
       </a>
       was discovered by
       <a href="/wiki/William_Lassell" title="William Lassell">
        William Lassell
       </a>
       in 1846, just seventeen days after the
       <a href="/wiki/Discovery_of_Neptune" title="Discovery of Neptune">
        discovery of Neptune
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lassell1846_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lassell1846-3">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Nereid_(moon)" title="Nereid (moon)">
        Nereid
       </a>
       was discovered by
       <a href="/wiki/Gerard_Kuiper" title="Gerard Kuiper">
        Gerard P. Kuiper
       </a>
       in 1949.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuiper1949_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kuiper1949-4">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The third moon, later named
       <a href="/wiki/Larissa_(moon)" title="Larissa (moon)">
        Larissa
       </a>
       , was first observed by Harold J. Reitsema, William B. Hubbard, Larry A. Lebofsky and
       <a href="/wiki/David_J._Tholen" title="David J. Tholen">
        David J. Tholen
       </a>
       on May 24, 1981. The astronomers were observing a star's close approach to Neptune, looking for rings similar to
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Uranus" title="Rings of Uranus">
        those discovered around Uranus
       </a>
       four years earlier.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reitsema1982_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Reitsema1982-5">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       If rings were present, the star's luminosity would decrease slightly just before the planet's closest approach. The star's luminosity dipped only for several seconds, which meant that it was due to a moon rather than a ring.
      </p>
      <p>
       No further moons were found until
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">
         Voyager 2
        </a>
       </i>
       flew by Neptune in 1989.
       <i>
        Voyager 2
       </i>
       rediscovered Larissa and discovered five inner moons:
       <a href="/wiki/Naiad_(moon)" title="Naiad (moon)">
        Naiad
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Thalassa_(moon)" title="Thalassa (moon)">
        Thalassa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Despina_(moon)" title="Despina (moon)">
        Despina
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Galatea_(moon)" title="Galatea (moon)">
        Galatea
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Proteus_(moon)" title="Proteus (moon)">
        Proteus
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith1989_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith1989-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2001 two surveys using large ground-based telescopes found five additional outer moons, bringing the total to thirteen.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HolmanKavelaarsGrav2004_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-HolmanKavelaarsGrav2004-7">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Follow-up surveys by two teams in 2002 and 2003 respectively re-observed all five of these moons, which are
       <a href="/wiki/Halimede_(moon)" title="Halimede (moon)">
        Halimede
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sao_(moon)" title="Sao (moon)">
        Sao
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Psamathe_(moon)" title="Psamathe (moon)">
        Psamathe
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Laomedeia" title="Laomedeia">
        Laomedeia
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Neso_(moon)" title="Neso (moon)">
        Neso
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HolmanKavelaarsGrav2004_7-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-HolmanKavelaarsGrav2004-7">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SheppardJewittKleyna2006_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SheppardJewittKleyna2006-8">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A sixth candidate moon was also found in the 2002 survey and was lost thereafter: it may have been a
       <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">
        centaur
       </a>
       instead of a satellite, although its small amount of motion relative to Neptune over a month suggests that it was indeed a satellite.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HolmanKavelaarsGrav2004_7-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-HolmanKavelaarsGrav2004-7">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was estimated to have a diameter of 33 km and to have been about 25.1 million km (0.168
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">
        AU
       </a>
       ) from Neptune when it was found.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HolmanKavelaarsGrav2004_7-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-HolmanKavelaarsGrav2004-7">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Names">
        Names
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Neptune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Names">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Name_conflicts_with_minor_planets" title="Name conflicts with minor planets">
        Name conflicts with minor planets
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Triton did not have an official name until the twentieth century. The name "Triton" was suggested by
       <a href="/wiki/Camille_Flammarion" title="Camille Flammarion">
        Camille Flammarion
       </a>
       in his 1880 book
       <i>
        Astronomie Populaire
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but it did not come into common use until at least the 1930s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Until this time it was usually simply known as "the satellite of Neptune". Other moons of Neptune are also named for Greek and Roman
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_water_deities" title="List of water deities">
        water gods
       </a>
       , in keeping with
       <a href="/wiki/Neptune_(mythology)" title="Neptune (mythology)">
        Neptune
       </a>
       's position as god of the sea:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazetteer_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gazetteer-11">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       either from
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">
        Greek mythology
       </a>
       , usually children of
       <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">
        Poseidon
       </a>
       , the Greek Neptune (Triton, Proteus, Despina, Thalassa); lovers of Poseidon (Larissa); classes of minor Greek water deities (
       <a href="/wiki/Naiad" title="Naiad">
        Naiad
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Nereid" title="Nereid">
        Nereid
       </a>
       ); or specific Nereids (Halimede, Galatea, Neso, Sao, Laomedeia, Psamathe).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazetteer_11-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gazetteer-11">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For the "normal" irregular satellites, the convention is to use names ending in "a" for prograde satellites, names ending in "e" for retrograde satellites, and names ending in "o" for exceptionally inclined satellites, exactly like the convention for the
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">
        moons of Jupiter
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Two
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
        asteroids
       </a>
       share the same names as moons of Neptune:
       <a href="/wiki/74_Galatea" title="74 Galatea">
        74 Galatea
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/1162_Larissa" title="1162 Larissa">
        1162 Larissa
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">
        Characteristics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Neptune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Characteristics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The moons of Neptune can be divided into two groups: regular and irregular. The first group includes the seven inner moons, which follow circular prograde orbits lying in the equatorial plane of Neptune. The second group consists of all seven other moons including Triton. They generally follow inclined eccentric and often retrograde orbits far from Neptune; the only exception is Triton, which orbits close to the planet following a circular orbit, though retrograde and inclined.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2007_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2007-13">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">

      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Regular_moons">
        Regular moons
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Neptune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Regular moons">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In order of distance from Neptune, the regular moons are
       <a href="/wiki/Naiad_(moon)" title="Naiad (moon)">
        Naiad
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Thalassa_(moon)" title="Thalassa (moon)">
        Thalassa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Despina_(moon)" title="Despina (moon)">
        Despina
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Galatea_(moon)" title="Galatea (moon)">
        Galatea
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Larissa_(moon)" title="Larissa (moon)">
        Larissa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/S/2004_N_1" title="S/2004 N 1">
        S/2004 N 1
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Proteus_(moon)" title="Proteus (moon)">
        Proteus
       </a>
       . All but the outer two are within Neptune-
       <a href="/wiki/Synchronous_orbit" title="Synchronous orbit">
        synchronous orbit
       </a>
       (Neptune's rotational period is 0.6713 day
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-fact-14">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) and thus are being
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_deceleration" title="Tidal deceleration">
        tidally decelerated
       </a>
       . Naiad, the closest regular moon, is also the second smallest among the inner moons (following the discovery of S/2004 N 1), whereas Proteus is the largest regular moon and the second largest moon of Neptune.
      </p>
      <p>
       The inner moons are closely associated with
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Neptune" title="Rings of Neptune">
        Neptune's rings
       </a>
       . The two innermost satellites, Naiad and Thalassa, orbit between the
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Neptune#Inner_rings" title="Rings of Neptune">
        Galle
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Neptune#Inner_rings" title="Rings of Neptune">
        LeVerrier rings
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith1989_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith1989-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Despina may be a
       <a href="/wiki/Shepherd_moon" title="Shepherd moon">
        shepherd moon
       </a>
       of the LeVerrier ring, because its orbit lies just inside this ring.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miner2007b_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Miner2007b-15">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The next moon,
       <a href="/wiki/Galatea_(moon)" title="Galatea (moon)">
        Galatea
       </a>
       , orbits just inside the most prominent of Neptune's rings, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adams_ring" title="Adams ring">
        Adams ring
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miner2007b_15-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Miner2007b-15">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This ring is very narrow, with a width not exceeding 50 km,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horn1990_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Horn1990-16">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and has five embedded bright
       <a href="/wiki/Arc_(geometry)" title="Arc (geometry)">
        arcs
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miner2007b_15-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Miner2007b-15">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The gravity of Galatea helps confine the ring particles within a limited region in the radial direction, maintaining the narrow ring. Various
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">
        resonances
       </a>
       between the ring particles and Galatea may also have a role in maintaining the arcs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miner2007b_15-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Miner2007b-15">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Only the two largest regular moons have been imaged with a resolution sufficient to discern their shapes and surface features.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith1989_6-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith1989-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Larissa, about 200 km in diameter, is elongated. Proteus is not significantly elongated, but not fully spherical either:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith1989_6-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith1989-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       it resembles an irregular
       <a href="/wiki/Polyhedron" title="Polyhedron">
        polyhedron
       </a>
       , with several flat or slightly
       <a href="/wiki/Curved_mirror#Concave_mirrors" title="Curved mirror">
        concave
       </a>
       facets 150 to 250 km in diameter.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stooke1994_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stooke1994-17">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At about 400 km in diameter, it is larger than the Saturnian moon
       <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">
        Mimas
       </a>
       , which is fully ellipsoidal. This difference may be due to a past collisional disruption of Proteus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BanfieldMurray_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BanfieldMurray-18">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The surface of Proteus is heavily cratered and shows a number of linear features. Its largest crater, Pharos, is more than 150 km in diameter.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith1989_6-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith1989-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stooke1994_17-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stooke1994-17">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       All of Neptune's inner moons are dark objects: their
       <a href="/wiki/Geometric_albedo" title="Geometric albedo">
        geometric albedo
       </a>
       ranges from 7 to 10%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karkoschka2003_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Karkoschka2003-19">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Their
       <a href="/wiki/Spectrum" title="Spectrum">
        spectra
       </a>
       indicate that they are made from water ice contaminated by some very dark material, probably complex
       <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">
        organic compounds
       </a>
       . In this respect, the inner Neptunian moons are similar to the inner
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Uranus" title="Moons of Uranus">
        Uranian moons
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith1989_6-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith1989-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Irregular_moons">
        Irregular moons
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Neptune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Irregular moons">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheIrregulars_NEPTUNE.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/TheIrregulars_NEPTUNE.svg/300px-TheIrregulars_NEPTUNE.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/TheIrregulars_NEPTUNE.svg/450px-TheIrregulars_NEPTUNE.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/TheIrregulars_NEPTUNE.svg/600px-TheIrregulars_NEPTUNE.svg.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TheIrregulars_NEPTUNE.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The diagram illustrates the orbits of Neptune's
         <a href="/wiki/Irregular_moon" title="Irregular moon">
          irregular moons
         </a>
         excluding Triton. The eccentricity is represented by the yellow segments extending from the
         <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">
          pericenter to apocenter
         </a>
         with the inclination represented on Y axis. The moons above the X axis are
         <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_and_prograde_motion" title="Retrograde and prograde motion">
          prograde
         </a>
         , those beneath are
         <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_and_prograde_motion" title="Retrograde and prograde motion">
          retrograde
         </a>
         . The X axis is labeled in
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigametre" title="Gigametre">
          Gm
         </a>
         and the fraction of the
         <a href="/wiki/Hill_sphere" title="Hill sphere">
          Hill sphere
         </a>
         's radius.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In order of their distance from the planet, the irregular moons are
       <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">
        Triton
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Nereid_(moon)" title="Nereid (moon)">
        Nereid
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Halimede_(moon)" title="Halimede (moon)">
        Halimede
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sao_(moon)" title="Sao (moon)">
        Sao
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Laomedeia" title="Laomedeia">
        Laomedeia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Psamathe_(moon)" title="Psamathe (moon)">
        Psamathe
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Neso_(moon)" title="Neso (moon)">
        Neso
       </a>
       , a group that includes both prograde and retrograde objects.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2007_13-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2007-13">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The five outermost moons are similar to the irregular moons of other
       <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">
        giant planets
       </a>
       , and are thought to have been gravitationally captured by Neptune, unlike the regular satellites, which probably formed
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/In_situ" title="In situ">
         in situ
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SheppardJewittKleyna2006_8-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-SheppardJewittKleyna2006-8">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Triton and Nereid are unusual irregular satellites and are thus treated separately from the other five irregular Neptunian moons, which are more like the outer irregular satellites of the other outer planets.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SheppardJewittKleyna2006_8-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-SheppardJewittKleyna2006-8">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Firstly, they are the largest two known irregular moons in the Solar System, with Triton being almost an
       <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">
        order of magnitude
       </a>
       larger than all other known irregular moons. Secondly, they both have atypically small semi-major axes, with Triton's being over an order of magnitude smaller than those of all other known irregular moons. Thirdly, they both have unusual orbital eccentricities: Nereid has one of the most eccentric orbits of any known irregular satellite, and Triton's orbit is a nearly perfect circle. Finally, Nereid also has the lowest inclination of any known irregular satellite.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SheppardJewittKleyna2006_8-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-SheppardJewittKleyna2006-8">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Triton">
        Triton
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Neptune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Triton">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">
        Triton (moon)
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Triton_orbit_%26_Neptune.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="765" data-file-width="1600" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Triton_orbit_%26_Neptune.png/300px-Triton_orbit_%26_Neptune.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Triton_orbit_%26_Neptune.png/450px-Triton_orbit_%26_Neptune.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Triton_orbit_%26_Neptune.png/600px-Triton_orbit_%26_Neptune.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Triton_orbit_%26_Neptune.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The orbit of
         <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">
          Triton
         </a>
         (red) is different from most moons' orbit (green) in the orbit's direction, and the orbit is
         <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">
          tilted −23°
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Triton follows a retrograde and quasi-circular orbit, and is thought to be a gravitationally captured satellite. It was the second moon in the Solar System that was discovered to have a substantial
       <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">
        atmosphere
       </a>
       , which is primarily
       <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
        nitrogen
       </a>
       with small amounts of
       <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
        methane
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">
        carbon monoxide
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElliotStrobelZhu_2000_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ElliotStrobelZhu_2000-20">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The pressure on Triton's surface is about 14
       <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">
        μbar
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElliotStrobelZhu_2000_20-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ElliotStrobelZhu_2000-20">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1989 the
       <i>
        Voyager 2
       </i>
       spacecraft observed what appeared to be clouds and hazes in this thin atmosphere.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith1989_6-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith1989-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Triton is one of the coldest bodies in the Solar System, with a surface temperature of about 38 K (−235.2 °C).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ElliotStrobelZhu_2000_20-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ElliotStrobelZhu_2000-20">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its surface is covered by nitrogen, methane,
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
        carbon dioxide
       </a>
       and water
       <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">
        ices
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cruikshank1993_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cruikshank1993-21">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and has a high
       <a href="/wiki/Geometric_albedo" title="Geometric albedo">
        geometric albedo
       </a>
       of more than 70%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith1989_6-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith1989-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Bond_albedo" title="Bond albedo">
        Bond albedo
       </a>
       is even higher, reaching up to 90%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith1989_6-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith1989-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [note 2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Surface features include the large southern
       <a href="/wiki/Polar_ice_cap" title="Polar ice cap">
        polar cap
       </a>
       , older cratered planes cross-cut by
       <a href="/wiki/Graben" title="Graben">
        graben
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Escarpment" title="Escarpment">
        scarps
       </a>
       , as well as youthful features probably formed by endogenic processes like
       <a href="/wiki/Cryovolcano" title="Cryovolcano">
        cryovolcanism
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith1989_6-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith1989-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Voyager 2
       </i>
       observations revealed a number of active
       <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">
        geysers
       </a>
       within the polar cap heated by the Sun, which eject plumes to the height of up to 8 km.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith1989_6-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith1989-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Triton has a relatively high density of about 2 g/cm
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       indicating that
       <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">
        rocks
       </a>
       constitute about two thirds of its mass, and ices (mainly water ice) the remaining one third. There may be a layer of liquid water deep inside Triton, forming a subterranean ocean.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussmann2006_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hussmann2006-23">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Because of its retrograde orbit and relative proximity to Neptune (closer than the Moon is to Earth),
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_deceleration" title="Tidal deceleration">
        tidal deceleration
       </a>
       is causing Triton to spiral inward, which will lead to its destruction in about 3.6 billion years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chyba_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chyba-24">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Nereid">
        Nereid
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Neptune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Nereid">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Nereid_(moon)" title="Nereid (moon)">
        Nereid (moon)
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Nereid is the third-largest moon of Neptune. It has a prograde but very eccentric orbit and is believed to be a former regular satellite that was scattered to its current orbit through gravitational interactions during Triton's capture.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldreich1989_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Goldreich1989-25">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Water ice has been spectroscopically detected on its surface. Early measurements of Nereid showed large, irregular variations in its visible magnitude, which were speculated to be caused by forced
       <a href="/wiki/Precession" title="Precession">
        precession
       </a>
       or chaotic rotation combined with an elongated shape and bright or dark spots on the surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shaefer2008_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Shaefer2008-26">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This was disproved in 2016, when observations from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kepler_space_telescope" title="Kepler space telescope">
        Kepler space telescope
       </a>
       showed only minor variations. Thermal modeling based on infrared observations from the
       <a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">
        Spitzer
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Herschel_Space_Observatory" title="Herschel Space Observatory">
        Herschel
       </a>
       space telescopes suggest that Nereid is only moderately elongated which disfavours forced precession of the rotation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-27">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The thermal model also indicates that the surface roughness of Nereid is very high, likely similar to the Saturnian moon
       <a href="/wiki/Hyperion_(moon)" title="Hyperion (moon)">
        Hyperion
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_27-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-27">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Normal_irregular_moons">
        Normal irregular moons
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Neptune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Normal irregular moons">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Among the remaining irregular moons, Sao and Laomedeia follow prograde orbits, whereas Halimede, Psamathe and Neso follow retrograde orbits. Given the similarity of their orbits, it was suggested that Neso and Psamathe could have a common origin in the break-up of a larger moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SheppardJewittKleyna2006_8-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-SheppardJewittKleyna2006-8">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Psamathe and Neso have the largest orbits of any natural satellites discovered in the Solar system to date. They take 25 years to orbit Neptune at an average of 125 times the distance between Earth and the Moon. Neptune has the largest
       <a href="/wiki/Hill_sphere" title="Hill sphere">
        Hill sphere
       </a>
       in the Solar System, owing primarily to its large distance from the Sun; this allows it to retain control of such distant moons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2007_13-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2007-13">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nevertheless, Jupiter's
       <a href="/wiki/S/2003_J_2" title="S/2003 J 2">
        S/2003 J 2
       </a>
       orbits at the greatest percentage of the primary's Hill radius of all the moons in the Solar System on average, and the Jovian moons in the
       <a href="/wiki/Carme_group" title="Carme group">
        Carme
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Pasiphae_group" title="Pasiphae group">
        Pasiphae groups
       </a>
       orbit at a greater percentage of their primary's Hill radius than Psamathe and Neso.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2007_13-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2007-13">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">
        Formation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Neptune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Formation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The mass distribution of the Neptunian moons is the most lopsided of the satellite systems of the
       <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">
        giant planets
       </a>
       in the Solar System. One moon, Triton, makes up nearly all of the mass of the system, with all other moons together comprising only one third of one percent. This is similar to the moon system of Saturn, where Titan makes up more than 95% of the total mass. This may be because Triton was captured well after the formation of Neptune's original satellite system, much of which would have been destroyed in the process of capture.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldreich1989_25-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Goldreich1989-25">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naeye2006_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Naeye2006-28">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [note 3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Masa_de_triton.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="1052" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Masa_de_triton.svg/220px-Masa_de_triton.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Masa_de_triton.svg/330px-Masa_de_triton.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Masa_de_triton.svg/440px-Masa_de_triton.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Masa_de_triton.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The relative masses of the Neptunian moons
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Triton's orbit upon capture would have been highly eccentric, and would have caused chaotic perturbations in the orbits of the original inner Neptunian satellites, causing them to collide and reduce to a disc of rubble.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldreich1989_25-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Goldreich1989-25">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This means it is likely that Neptune's present inner satellites are not the original bodies that formed with Neptune. Only after Triton's orbit became circularised could some of the rubble re-accrete into the present-day regular moons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BanfieldMurray_18-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-BanfieldMurray-18">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This great perturbation may possibly be the reason why the satellite system of Neptune does not follow the 10,000:1 ratio of mass between the parent planet and all its moons seen in the satellite systems of all the other giant planets.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naeye2006b_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Naeye2006b-30">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The mechanism of Triton’s capture has been the subject of several theories over the years. One of them postulates that Triton was captured in a
       <a href="/wiki/Three-body_problem" title="Three-body problem">
        three-body
       </a>
       encounter. In this scenario, Triton is the surviving member of a
       <a href="/wiki/Binary_asteroid" title="Binary asteroid">
        binary
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">
        Kuiper belt
       </a>
       object
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [note 4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       disrupted by its encounter with Neptune.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agnor2006_31-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Agnor2006-31">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Numerical simulations show that there is a 0.41 probability that the moon Halimede collided with Nereid at some time in the past.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HolmanKavelaarsGrav2004_7-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-HolmanKavelaarsGrav2004-7">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although it is not known whether any collision has taken place, both moons appear to have similar ("grey") colors, implying that Halimede could be a fragment of Nereid.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GravHolmanFraser2004_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-GravHolmanFraser2004-33">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="List">
        List
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Neptune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: List">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="wikitable" style="float: right; margin-right: 0;">
       <caption>
        Key
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <td bgcolor="#CCCCFF" style="text-align: center;" width="50">

         </sup>

        </td>
        <td bgcolor="#F4C2C2" style="text-align: center;" width="50">
         <sup>
          ♠
         </sup>
         <br/>
         Retrograde irregular moons
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The Neptunian moons are listed here by orbital period, from shortest to longest. Irregular (captured) moons are marked by color. Triton, the only Neptunian moon massive enough for its surface to have
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_collapse" title="Gravitational collapse">
        collapsed
       </a>
       into a
       <a href="/wiki/Spheroid" title="Spheroid">
        spheroid
       </a>
       , is bolded.
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
        Notes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Moons_of_Neptune&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Notes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
       <ol class="references">
        <li id="cite_note-1">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-1">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          This is a IAU guideline that will be followed at the naming of every Neptunian moon, although one (
          <a href="/wiki/S/2004_N_1" title="S/2004 N 1">
           S/2004 N 1
          </a>
          ) has yet to receive a permanent name.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-22">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-22">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          The geometric albedo of an astronomical body is the ratio of its actual brightness at zero
          <a href="/wiki/Phase_angle_(astronomy)" title="Phase angle (astronomy)">
           phase angle
          </a>
          (i.e. as seen from the light source) to that of an idealized flat, fully reflecting,
          <a href="/wiki/Diffuse_reflection" title="Diffuse reflection">
           diffusively scattering
          </a>
          (
          <a href="/wiki/Lambertian_reflectance" title="Lambertian reflectance">
           Lambertian
          </a>
          ) disk with the same cross-section. The Bond albedo, named after the American astronomer
          <a href="/wiki/George_Phillips_Bond" title="George Phillips Bond">
           George Phillips Bond
          </a>
          (1825–1865), who originally proposed it, is the fraction of
          <a href="/wiki/Power_(physics)" title="Power (physics)">
           power
          </a>
          in the total
          <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">
           electromagnetic radiation
          </a>
          incident on an astronomical body that is scattered back out into space. The Bond albedo is a value strictly between 0 and 1, as it includes all possible scattered light (but not radiation from the body itself). This is in contrast to other definitions of
          <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
           albedo
          </a>
          such as the geometric albedo, which can be above 1. In general, though, the Bond albedo may be greater or smaller than the geometric albedo, depending on surface and atmospheric properties of the body in question.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-29">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-29">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Saturn" title="Moons of Saturn">
           Saturn's satellite system
          </a>
          is the next most lopsided, with most of its mass being in its largest moon
          <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
           Titan
          </a>
          .
          <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">
           Jupiter
          </a>
          and
          <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Uranus" title="Moons of Uranus">
           Uranus
          </a>
          have more balanced systems.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-32">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-32">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <a href="/wiki/Minor-planet_moon#Trans-Neptunian_objects" title="Minor-planet moon">
           Binary objects
          </a>
          , objects with moons such as the
          <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">
           Pluto
          </a>
          –
          <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">
           Charon
          </a>
          system, are quite common among the larger
          <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">
           trans-Neptunian objects
          </a>
          (TNOs). Around 11% of all TNOs may be binaries.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agnor2006_31-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-Agnor2006-31">
            [28]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-34">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-34">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Order refers to the position among other moons with respect to their average distance from Neptune.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-35">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-35">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Label refers to the
          <a href="/wiki/Roman_numerals" title="Roman numerals">
           Roman numeral
          </a>
          attributed to each moon in order of their discovery.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazetteer_11-2">
           <a href="#cite_note-Gazetteer-11">
            [10]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-38">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-38">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Diameters with multiple entries such as "60×40×34" reflect that the body is not spherical and that each of its dimensions has been measured well enough to provide a 3-axis estimate. The dimensions of the five inner moons were taken from Karkoschka, 2003.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karkoschka2003_19-1">
           <a href="#cite_note-Karkoschka2003-19">
            [18]
           </a>
          </sup>
          Dimensions of Proteus are from Stooke (1994).
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stooke1994_17-2">
           <a href="#cite_note-Stooke1994-17">
            [16]
           </a>
          </sup>
          Dimensions of Triton are from Thomas, 2000,
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2000_36-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-Thomas2000-36">
            [30]
           </a>
          </sup>
          whereas its diameter is taken from Davies et al., 1991.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies1991_37-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-Davies1991-37">
            [31]
           </a>
          </sup>
          The size of Nereid is from Smith, 1989.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith1989_6-11">
           <a href="#cite_note-Smith1989-6">
            [5]
           </a>
          </sup>
          The sizes of the outer moons are from Sheppard et al., 2006.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SheppardJewittKleyna2006_8-5">
           <a href="#cite_note-SheppardJewittKleyna2006-8">
            [7]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-39">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-39">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Mass of all moons of Neptune except Triton were calculated assuming a density of 1.3 g/cm
          <sup>
           3
          </sup>
          . The volumes of Larissa and Proteus were taken from Stooke (1994).
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stooke1994_17-3">
           <a href="#cite_note-Stooke1994-17">
            [16]
           </a>
          </sup>
          The mass of Triton is from Jacobson, 2009.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-41">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-41">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Each moon's inclination is given relative to its local
          <a href="/wiki/Laplace_plane" title="Laplace plane">
           Laplace plane
          </a>
          . Inclinations greater than 90° indicate
          <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_and_prograde_motion" title="Retrograde and prograde motion">
           retrograde orbits
          </a>
          (in the direction opposite to the planet's rotation).
         </span>
        </li>
       </ol>
      </div>

          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Hubble+Finds+New+Neptune+Moon&amp;rft.pub=Space+Telescope+Science+Institute&amp;rft.date=2013-07-15&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fhubblesite.org%2Fnewscenter%2Farchive%2Freleases%2F2013%2F30%2Ffull%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMoons+of+Neptune">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
       </ol>
      </div>
